[0:0:400,327] Natural 

[1:0:379,359] Resources 

[2:0:344,401] Natural Resources Agency programs protect and 

[2:1:344,411] restore California's diverse natural and cultural 

[2:2:344,421] resources for current and future generations, 

[3:0:344,431] including operating state parks, managing water 

[3:1:344,441] supplies, supporting forest health, encouraging 

[3:2:344,451] clean energy, and preserving fish and wildlife 

[3:3:344,461] habitats, rich farmlands and mineral resources. 

[3:4:344,471] Agency programs also protect the public through 

[3:5:344,481] suppression of wildfires and construction of levees 

[3:6:344,490] in the Central Valley. These programs not only 

[3:7:344,500] contribute to the state's unique quality of life, but 

[3:8:344,510] also are critical to sustaining a vibrant economy.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   1 

[1:0:214,43] 3100   California Science Center 

[2:0:66,64] The Science Center, the Office of Exposition Park Management and the California African American Museum (CAAM), are 

[2:1:66,74] located in Exposition Park, a 160-acre tract in south Los Angeles, which is owned by the state. For budget purposes, these 

[2:2:66,84] three departments are collectively known as the California Science Center. 

[3:0:66,102] The mission of the Science Center is to stimulate curiosity and inspire science related learning. The Office of Exposition Park 

[3:1:66,112] Management provides long-term leadership in the development and implementation of park usage policy and day-to-day 

[3:2:66,123] management, operation and promotion of the park for its tenants and the public. The mission of CAAM is to research, collect, 

[3:3:66,133] preserve and interpret for public enrichment, the history, art and culture of African Americans with a multi-layered focus that is 

[3:4:66,143] global, national, and local with special emphasis on California and the western United States.

[6:0:66,340] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[6:1:66,358] 2300 - EDUCATION 

[7:0:66,376] Attracting over 2 million guests annually, the Science Center-Education represents one of California's premier educational and 

[7:1:66,386] family destinations. Governed by a nine-member Board of Directors appointed by the Governor, the Science Center develops 

[7:2:66,396] and features award-winning exhibits and internationally renowned education programs. 

[8:0:66,414] Hands-on educational exhibits and programs focus on science, math, technology, and conservation which explore biological 

[8:1:66,424] processes of humans, animals, plants, the Earth's ecosystems, habitats, and geophysical processes, as well as engineering, 

[8:2:66,434] communications, and transportation, on land and in space. In 2012, the Space Shuttle Endeavour was moved to its temporary 

[8:3:66,444] home - the Samuel Oschin Pavilion - while design and construction of the Science Center's Air and Space Center - its 

[8:4:66,454] permanent home - is completed. 

[9:0:66,472] In addition, the Science Center operates a 3D IMAX theater which features science related films and documentaries. The 

[9:1:66,482] Center for Science Learning offers professional development programs to improve math and science skills of teachers and 

[9:2:66,493] other educators. The Science Center School is a K-5 science, math and technology focused neighborhood charter school. As a 

[9:3:66,503] Title I school, it serves one of the more underserved and economically challenged communities in South Los Angeles. The 

[9:4:66,513] school's instructional programs and teacher training meet California science standards. 

[10:0:66,531] The Science Center's mission values accessibility and inclusiveness and strives to inspire interest in science among those 

[10:1:66,541] traditionally underrepresented in science, math, technology and engineering. The Science Center receives significant funding 

[10:2:66,551] support for exhibit development and capital outlay projects from the California Science Center Foundation. 

[10:3:66,569] 2305 - EXPOSITION PARK MANAGEMENT 

[11:0:66,586] The Office of Exposition Park Management (OEPM) is responsible for public safety operations, parking services management 

[11:1:66,597] and overall park beautification maintenance. In addition, OEPM administers supervision and coordination of park-wide events, 

[11:2:66,607] and facilitates communication efforts between state, city, county, and private entities represented within the park. OEPM is 

[11:3:66,617] responsible for the negotiations of ground leases and agreements which impact the state's interest, including development of 

[11:4:66,627] park facilities and future infrastructure improvements. OEPM is also responsible for managing the Exposition Park 

[12:0:66,637] Improvement Fund and generating revenue to maintain the park. Exposition Park enriches the internal park community and in 

[12:1:66,648] addition provides green space and recreational areas for the surrounding neighborhood. 

[12:2:66,665] 2310 - CALIFORNIA AFRICAN AMERICAN MUSEUM 

[13:0:66,683] The California African American Museum (CAAM) is governed by a seven-member board of directors appointed by the 

[13:1:66,693] Governor. CAAM's mission is history, art and culture. The diverse exhibits are displayed on a seasonal basis in the various 

[13:2:66,703] galleries of the 44,000 square foot building. CAAM delivers its mission to the public through its history and art collections; 

[13:3:66,714] permanent, self-curated, temporary and traveling exhibits; and, innumerable programs and workshops, lectures, seminars, 

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:2:66,85] Buses & Docents; CAAM Technology Arts Program; employment skill development for high school students under Young

[3:4:66,106] are Learning; Professional Educator's Open Houses; Films & Shorts at CAAM; off-site workshops and professionally led 

[3:5:66,116] classes in Learning Without Boundaries; and, family and cross-cultural community presentations through Target Sundays at 

[3:6:66,126] CAAM. For these and many other services CAAM is also generously supported with contributions from its 501(c)(3) partner 

[3:7:66,136] Friends, the Foundation of the California African American Museum.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   10   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:201,43] 3125   California Tahoe Conservancy 

[1:1:66,64] The California Tahoe Conservancy's mission is to lead California's efforts to restore and enhance the extraordinary natural and 

[1:2:66,74] recreational resources of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy achieves its mission by developing and implementing 

[1:3:66,84] programs that acquire environmentally sensitive land, provide grants or directly fund soil erosion prevention and watershed 

[1:4:66,95] restoration, improve stream environments and wildlife habitats, and improve water quality. The Conservancy also balances 

[1:5:66,105] these activities with programs to improve public access and recreational opportunities in an environmentally sound manner by 

[1:6:66,115] acquiring lakefront property, constructing pedestrian and bike paths, and building public access facilities. 

[2:0:66,133] The Conservancy is also a key implementing agency of the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) which is a 1997 

[2:1:66,143] agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local governments, and 

[2:2:66,153] various private entities to commit $908 million over 10 years to a list of acquisition and capital improvement projects needed to 

[2:3:66,163] achieve environmental goals in the Basin. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects, 

[2:4:66,174] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and inclusion of new projects. The 2008-2018 EIP was finalized in July 2009. 

[2:5:66,191] Because the Conservancy's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Conservancy has a capital outlay program 

[2:6:66,201] to support this need. For the specifics on the Conservancy's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[7:0:66,426] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[7:1:66,445] 2340 - TAHOE CONSERVANCY 

[8:0:66,465] The California Tahoe Conservancy performs day-to-day management and maintenance of the lands it has acquired, as well as 

[8:1:66,475] the completion of corrective and restorative actions needed to fully achieve the purposes of the acquisitions. It is envisioned 

[8:2:66,485] that the Conservancy will acquire up to 6,000 environmentally sensitive parcels under existing criteria. To date, nearly 4,900 

[8:3:66,496] parcels, totaling nearly 6,500 acres, have been acquired. These parcels are being managed to maintain and/or restore their 

[8:4:66,506] natural functions, provide for public safety, and provide for open spaces. 

[9:0:66,526] The Conservancy has also acquired additional parcels where more extensive site improvements or more active ultimate uses 

[9:1:66,536] are envisioned. For example, the management objectives for the public access and recreation lands are to allow for: suitable 

[9:2:66,547] and appropriate interim uses during the site improvement planning and implementation process, design and construction of 

[9:3:66,557] public access facilities, and long-term management. 

[10:0:66,577] In addition, high rates of tree mortality (25 - 40 percent) throughout the Basin have required special emphasis on forest 

[10:1:66,587] resource management activities to reduce fuel hazards, remove hazardous conditions, start corrective actions to manage forest 

[10:2:66,598] health through a variety of means, and limit public liability. Activities include the removal of diseased, dead and dying trees to 

[10:3:66,608] promote forest health while reducing the risk of fire that would destroy property and habitat, threaten lives, and diminish the 

[10:4:66,618] water quality and other resource values of Lake Tahoe. 

[11:0:66,638] The program also provides grants to local governments to improve the Lake's water quality by controlling soil erosion caused 

[11:1:66,649] by existing development, primarily along roadways, and to treat sediment-laden runoff. These projects involve acquisitions and 

[11:2:66,659] site improvements such as the revegetation of barren slopes, and building roadside and storm drainage facilities and sediment 

[11:3:66,669] basins. 

[12:0:66,687] 2345 - ACQUISITION AND SITE IMPROVEMENT PROJECTS 

[13:0:66,704] This program acquires environmentally sensitive lands and provides public access and recreation by enhancing existing 

[13:1:66,715] facilities and establishing new public access sites along the Lake and in natural areas. The program also preserves a wide 

[14:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[6:0:66,275] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[7:0:66,293] The California Tahoe Conservancy manages programs to help protect Lake Tahoe's water quality and conserve wildlife habitat, 

[7:1:66,303] watershed areas, and public access on the California side of the Lake Tahoe Basin. The Conservancy is a primary implementer 

[7:2:66,313] of the State of California's responsibilities under the Environmental Improvement Program (EIP) for the Lake Tahoe Basin - a 

[7:3:66,323] 1997 agreement between California, Nevada, the Tahoe Regional Planning Agency, the federal government, local 

[7:4:66,334] governments, and various private entities. The EIP is updated periodically to include more refined estimates of projects, 

[7:5:66,344] modifications in the scope of identified projects, and new projects.

[1:0:188,43] 3210   Environmental Protection Program 

[1:1:66,64] The California Environmental Protection Program receives revenue from the sale of personalized motor vehicle license plates 

[1:2:66,74] by the Department of Motor Vehicles. Revenues are deposited in the California Environmental License Plate Fund. Funds 

[1:3:66,84] expended pursuant to Division 13.5 of the Public Resources Code must have one or more of the following purposes: 

[1:4:65,102] •   The control and abatement of air pollution, including all phases of research into the sources, dynamics and effects of 

[1:5:76,112] environmental pollutants. 

[2:0:65,129] •   The acquisition, preservation, restoration, or any combination thereof, of natural areas or ecological reserves. 

[2:1:65,145] •   Environmental education, including formal school programs and informal public education programs. 

[2:2:65,161] •   Protection of nongame species and threatened and endangered plants and animals. 

[2:3:65,177] •   Protection, enhancement, and restoration of fish and wildlife habitat and related water quality, including review of the 

[2:4:76,187] potential impact of development activities and land use changes on that habitat. 

[2:5:65,204] •   The purchase, on an opportunity basis, of real property consisting of sensitive natural areas for the state park system and for 

[2:6:76,214] local and regional parks. 

[3:0:65,230] •   Reduction or minimization of the effects of soil erosion and the discharge of sediment into the waters of the Lake Tahoe 

[3:1:76,240] region, including the restoration of disturbed wetlands and stream environment zones. 

[4:0:66,268] The appropriations and expenditures for programs and projects funded from the California Environmental License Plate Fund 

[4:1:66,278] are contained in the budgets of the various state boards, commissions and departments.

[1:0:200,43] 3340   California Conservation Corps 

[2:0:66,64] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) provides young women and men the opportunity to work hard responding to fires, 

[2:1:66,74] floods and other disasters, restoring California's environment, and installing clean energy and energy conservation measures at 

[2:2:66,84] public facilities throughout the state. Through their service, the corpsmembers gain life, work, and academic skills to become 

[2:3:66,95] strong workers and citizens. 

[3:0:66,112] In addition to the CCC, there are also 14 local conservation corps located in metropolitan communities throughout the state that 

[3:1:66,123] are annually certified by the CCC, and engage young people in conservation, recycling, education, and training activities. 

[3:2:66,140] Because CCC's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to support 

[3:3:66,150] this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[5:0:66,428] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[6:0:66,447] 2360 - TRAINING AND WORK PROGRAM 

[6:1:66,464] This Program focuses on four areas: 

[7:0:66,482] Natural Resource Work: Corpsmembers protect and enhance the state's natural resources through park development, trail 

[7:1:66,492] construction, tree planting, fire hazard reduction, watershed improvement, wildlife habitat enhancement, removal of non-native 

[7:2:66,502] vegetation, meadow restoration, energy and water auditing and retrofitting, irrigation system installation, and drought-tolerant 

[7:3:66,513] and other landscaping. 

[8:0:66,530] Disaster Response: Corpsmembers are dispatched to fires, assisting with initial attack, mop-up and logistical support; floods, 

[8:1:66,540] filling sandbags, reinforcing levees and stabilizing hillsides; earthquakes, removing hazards and staffing disaster assistance 

[8:2:66,551] centers; oil spill clean-up; snow removal; search and rescue operations; pest infestation eradication; and homeland security 

[8:3:66,561] assistance. 

[9:0:66,579] Corpsmember Education: Corpsmembers are provided opportunities to advance their academic skills while in the CCC through 

[9:1:66,589] local adult and charter schools and community colleges. 

[10:0:66,606] Corpsmember Development and Training: The CCC stresses the development of both a work and service ethic, which includes 

[10:1:66,617] teamwork, self-discipline, leadership, and giving back to California. Corpsmembers learn conservation principles and career 

[10:2:66,627] planning. The CCC also offers training in trail building, first aid, Hazardous Waste Operations and Emergency Response, and 

[10:3:66,637] firefighting certification, which can lead to internship opportunities with various employers in California.

[8:0:66,514] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[9:0:66,532] The California Conservation Corps (CCC) operates 26 facilities statewide, including 8 residential and 18 non-residential 

[9:1:66,542] facilities in both urban and rural areas. These facilities help the CCC achieve its mission of providing employment, training, and 

[9:2:66,553] educational opportunities to young men and women, and assisting federal, state and local agencies, and nonprofit entities with 

[9:3:66,563] the conservation of California's natural resources. 

[10:0:66,583] The CCC residential facilities house 80-100 corpsmembers and operate 24 hours a day, bringing young people together from 

[10:1:66,593] across the state, increasing capacity for emergency response and conservation projects. Non-residential centers range in size 

[10:2:66,604] from 30-60 corpsmembers and allow young people with families to live at home and report to work.

[1:0:105,43] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission 

[2:0:66,64] The Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission (Energy Commission) is responsible for ensuring a 

[3:0:66,74] reliable supply of energy to meet state needs while protecting public health, safety, and the environment. Activities include the 

[3:1:66,84] following: making energy public policy recommendations; collecting targeted energy data and ensuring data is managed 

[4:0:66,95] responsibly; developing and implementing research, development, demonstration, and deployment policies and programs that 

[4:1:66,105] promote strategic energy investments; ensuring energy facilities approved by the Energy Commission are designed, 

[5:0:66,115] constructed, operated, and decommissioned in compliance with all applicable laws, ordinances, regulations, and standards; 

[5:1:66,125] adopting building and appliance efficiency standards; promoting development and deployment of advanced transportation 

[5:2:66,135] technology; and supporting climate change goals through integrated resource planning, renewable energy development, and 

[5:3:66,146] system integration.

[4:0:66,190] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:0:66,208] 2380 - REGULATORY and PLANNING PROGRAM 

[6:0:66,226] The Regulatory and Planning program facilitates markets in providing adequate statewide energy supplies. The program 

[6:1:66,236] certifies power plant sites, designates transmission line corridors, and monitors new energy facilities and fuel infrastructure for 

[6:2:66,246] compliance and supply and distribution adequacy consistent with state energy policies. Additionally, the Energy Assessments 

[6:3:66,257] program develops long-range projections for future energy supplies and demand, maintains current information on statewide 

[6:4:66,267] electrical generation sources, consumption and peak demand, natural gas, transportation fuels, and distributed generation. The 

[6:5:66,277] program develops state energy policy based on the economic, financial, security, safety, and environmental implications of 

[6:6:66,287] supply, demand, and price/cost assessments, and monitors energy markets for competitive prices and potential market abuses. 

[7:0:66,305] 2380010 - Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation Program 

[8:0:66,323] The Power Plant Site Certification and Transmission Line Corridor Designation program is responsible for the following: 

[8:1:66,333] reviewing power plant siting applications 50 megawatts and larger; ensuring power plant developers comply with conditions of 

[8:2:66,343] certification and all applicable laws and regulations when constructing and operating power plants; reviewing petitions to 

[8:3:66,353] amend siting decisions; reviewing applications for designation as transmission line corridors; evaluating constraints and 

[8:4:66,363] opportunities for energy resource development; and analyzing reliability, efficiency, and environmental performance issues 

[8:5:66,374] related to power plants and the state electricity transmission grid. 

[9:0:66,391] 2380019 - Electricity Analysis 

[10:0:66,409] The Electricity Analysis program is responsible for evaluating current and future electricity supply market trends and 

[10:1:66,419] infrastructure needs; estimating costs of various generation types; assessing the adequacy of electricity supplies; and 

[10:2:66,429] analyzing regulations and environmental issues related to electricity. Additionally, this program addresses the distribution 

[10:3:66,440] system and its relationship to distributed generation, including planning and interconnection barriers and provides information 

[10:4:66,450] and recommendations to state agencies, electricity market participants, and the public. The natural gas program component is 

[10:5:66,460] responsible for evaluating current and future natural gas market trends and infrastructure needs, assessing the adequacy of 

[10:6:66,470] natural gas supplies, monitoring the interaction of natural gas and electricity to maintain system reliability, analyzing regulations 

[10:7:66,480] and environmental issues related to natural gas, and leading interagency work groups to monitor short-term natural gas market 

[10:8:66,491] trends. The program also collects, monitors, and analyzes data on transportation fuel supply and production, fuel infrastructure 

[10:9:66,501] issues, and responds to energy and fuel shortages and emergencies. 

[11:0:66,519] 2385 - ENERGY RESOURCES CONSERVATION PROGRAM 

[12:0:66,536] The Energy Resources Conservation program objectives include reducing overall energy use and decreasing peak electricity 

[12:1:66,546] demand by identifying energy efficiency opportunities and developing and implementing programs and standards for efficiently 

[12:2:66,557] using energy in all sectors. 

[13:0:66,574] 2385010 - Building and Appliances Program 

[14:0:66,592] The Building and Appliances program objectives include reducing consumer and business energy use, water use, and peak 

[14:1:66,602] demand by developing and implementing strategies and minimum efficiency standards for appliances, equipment, and new and 

[14:2:66,612] existing residential and nonresidential buildings. The Building and Appliances program provides technical assistance on 

[14:3:66,623] building standards and enforcement support to the building industry and building departments. Further, the program maintains 

[14:4:66,633] a database of efficient appliances and equipment, and enforces compliance with the appliance efficiency standards. 

[15:0:66,651] 2385019 - Energy Projects Evaluation and Assistance Program 

[16:0:66,668] This program is responsible for providing technical assistance, grants, and loans to improve energy efficiency and reduce 

[16:1:66,678] operating costs of schools, colleges, local jurisdictions, hospitals, and public care facilities. 

[17:0:66,696] 2385028 - Demand Analysis 

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 36 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   34 

[1:0:454,24] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:70,38] 3360   Energy Resources Conservation and Development Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] The Demand Analysis program is responsible for collecting and analyzing electricity and natural gas consumption data used to 

[3:1:66,75] prepare energy demand forecasts, forecasting peak and total energy consumption by sector, and estimating the amount of 

[3:2:66,85] energy conserved by existing and proposed program activities. The program also performs transportation energy demand 

[3:3:66,95] forecasting, conducts consumer vehicle choice surveys, and assesses the impacts of transportation electrification on California 

[3:4:66,106] electricity use. 

[4:0:66,123] 2390 - DEVELOPMENT PROGRAM 

[5:0:66,141] The Development Program focuses on transportation and alternatives to conventional fossil fuels. The mission is to support 

[5:1:66,151] provision of adequate and reliable transportation energy to the California transportation sector while balancing economic, public 

[5:2:66,161] health, safety, and environmental consequences. The program also conducts research, development, demonstration, 

[5:3:66,172] commercialization, and deployment activities on energy efficiency, renewable and advanced energy technologies, and 

[5:4:66,182] alternative and renewable fuel and advanced vehicle technologies so that future energy supplies are cost effective, secure, and 

[5:5:66,192] reliable; enhance environmental quality; and promote state and local economic development. The program provides technical 

[5:6:66,202] assistance, financial assistance, direct technology research and demonstration, technology forecasting, technology analysis 

[5:7:66,212] and evaluation, and information transfer. 

[6:0:66,230] 2390010 - Transportation Technology and Fuels 

[7:0:66,248] The Transportation Technology and Fuels program provides financial incentives to develop and deploy innovative technologies 

[7:1:66,258] and strategies that transform California's fuel and vehicle types to help attain the state's climate change policies. The program 

[7:2:66,268] goal is the development and deployment of low-carbon alternative fuels and advanced vehicle technologies in the marketplace, 

[7:3:66,278] without adopting any one preferred fuel or technology. The program provides analyses and recommendations to guide state 

[7:4:66,289] energy policy and legislation to achieve climate change and air quality objectives, reduce petroleum consumption, and promote 

[7:5:66,299] economic development. The program also provides incentives to workforce development and training that intersect with the 

[7:6:66,309] respective program goals. 

[8:0:66,327] 2390019 - Research and Development 

[9:0:66,344] The Research and Development program provides analysis and investment recommendations for research, demonstration, 

[9:1:66,355] and development innovations addressing current and emerging energy system requirements. The program supports projects 

[9:2:66,365] that complement other private or public sector investments. The program goal is to develop, and help bring to market, energy 

[9:3:66,375] solutions providing increased environmental benefits, greater system reliability, and lower energy costs. Benefits are provided 

[9:4:66,385] to Californians through investments in energy efficiency and demand response, advanced generation, energy storage, 

[9:5:66,395] strategies to reduce or mitigate energy-related environmental impact, transportation technologies, and energy transmission and 

[9:6:66,406] distribution system improvements. 

[10:0:66,423] 2390028 - Renewable Energy 

[11:0:66,441] The Renewable Energy program fosters growth of the renewable energy market by providing financial incentives to support the 

[11:1:66,451] development and use of eligible renewable energy products; requiring utilities to disclose information to consumers on their 

[11:2:66,461] power fuel mix; implementing the Renewables Portfolio Standard; and establishing solar equipment rating standards and 

[11:3:66,472] maintaining solar equipment lists meeting the performance and safety standards. 

[12:0:66,489] 2395 - LOAN REPAYMENT PROGRAM 

[13:0:66,507] The Loan Repayment program consists of Conservation and Development program loan repayments deposited into the 

[13:1:66,517] following accounts: State Energy Conservation Assistance Account; Local Jurisdiction Energy Assistance Account; Energy 

[13:2:66,527] Technologies Research, Development and Demonstration Account; Local Government Geothermal Resources Revolving 

[13:3:66,538] Subaccount; and Clean and Renewable Energy Business Financing Revolving Loan Fund. 

[14:0:66,578] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM 

[15:0:418,599] 2016-17*   2017-18*   2018-19*

[2:0:66,64] The Colorado River Board protects California's rights and interests in the water and power resources of the Colorado River 

[2:1:66,74] system. The Board works with: Colorado River Basin states (Arizona, California, Colorado, Nevada, New Mexico, Utah, and 

[2:2:66,84] Wyoming), federal agencies, other state agencies, six local agencies (Palo Verde Irrigation District, Imperial Irrigation District, 

[2:3:66,95] Coachella Valley Water District, Metropolitan Water District of Southern California, San Diego County Water Authority, Los 

[2:4:66,105] Angeles Department of Water and Power), Congress, the courts, and Mexico. Its activities include analyses of engineering, 

[2:5:66,115] legal and economic matters concerning the Colorado River resources of the seven basin states and the 1944 United States-

[2:6:66,125] Mexico Water Treaty obligation to deliver Colorado River water to Mexico.

[3:0:75,68] Totals, Workload Budget Adjustments   $184   $52,242   0.4   $189   $33,891   28.7 

[3:1:75,82] Totals, Budget Adjustments   $184   $52,242   0.4   $189   $33,891   28.7 

[4:0:66,114] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[5:0:66,132] 2420 - GEOLOGIC HAZARDS AND MINERAL RESOURCES CONSERVATION 

[6:0:66,150] This program evaluates, assesses and maps the state's geologic and seismologic hazards, such as earthquakes, landslides, 

[6:1:66,160] tsunami and volcanic eruption threats, and hazardous minerals exposures, in order to protect the public health and safety and 

[6:2:66,170] the natural environment; analyzes the state's mineral assets and maps its mineral resources. Information is used by federal, 

[6:3:66,180] state, and local government agencies, industries and individual businesses, and the public to make informed decisions about 

[6:4:66,191] land use, seismic safety, and mineral development. 

[6:5:66,208] 2425 - OIL, GAS, AND GEOTHERMAL RESOURCES 

[7:0:66,226] This program regulates the drilling, operation, and abandonment of oil, natural gas, and geothermal wells to protect the 

[7:1:66,236] environment, prevent pollution, and ensure public safety. The state is fully reimbursed for program expenditures by annual 

[7:2:66,246] assessments and fees on the respective industries. Approximately 450 companies operate over 88,000 wells in California for 

[7:3:66,257] the production of oil, natural gas, and geothermal resources. 

[7:4:66,274] 2430 - LAND RESOURCE PROTECTION 

[8:0:66,292] This program protects agricultural farmland and open space through various financial incentives. Under the Land Conservation 

[8:1:66,302] Act, landowners who agree to keep their property undeveloped for at least ten years receive lower property tax assessments. 

[8:2:66,312] The California Farmland Conservancy Program provides grants to local governments and nonprofit land trusts for the 

[8:3:66,323] acquisition of agricultural conservation easements that permanently remove development rights, and therefore development 

[8:4:66,333] pressure, from agricultural lands. The Farmland Mapping and Monitoring Program within the Land Resource Protection 

[8:5:66,343] Program develops maps, statistics, and reports relating to farmland conversion, farmland inventory, and land protection to 

[8:6:66,353] assist in local land use decisions. 

[8:7:66,371] 2435 - MINE RECLAMATION 

[9:0:66,389] This program regulates active surface mining operations and monitors local lead agencies to ensure compliance with the 

[9:1:66,399] Surface Mining and Reclamation Act of 1975. It assists cities, counties, state agencies, and mine operators in their efforts to 

[9:2:66,409] reclaim mined lands to beneficial uses. This program also compiles an inventory of the state's estimated 47,000 abandoned 

[9:3:66,419] mines and remediates abandoned mine hazards to protect public safety. 

[9:4:66,437] 2440 - STATE MINING AND GEOLOGY BOARD 

[10:0:66,455] The Board serves as a regulatory and policy body for the state's geology, geologic and seismologic hazards, conservation of 

[10:1:66,465] mineral resources, and reclamation of mined lands. The Department's California Geological Survey and the Office of Mine 

[10:2:66,475] Reclamation provide the engineering, technical expertise, and support functions for the reports, plans, and maps which the 

[10:3:66,485] Board approves. The Board also serves as an appeals body for mining operations that have been issued notices of violations 

[10:4:66,495] or orders to comply, and disputes with local lead agencies. 

[11:0:66,536] DETAILED EXPENDITURES BY PROGRAM

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   68   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:164,43] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection 

[2:0:66,64] The California Department of Forestry and Fire Protection (CAL FIRE) serves and safeguards the people and protects the 

[2:1:66,74] property and resources of California. 

[3:0:66,92] CAL FIRE provides all hazard emergency - fire, medical, rescue and disaster - response to the public and provides leadership 

[3:1:66,102] in the protection of life, property and natural resources. 

[4:0:66,120] CAL FIRE safeguards the public through: engineering; research, development and adoption of regulations; fire and life safety 

[5:0:66,130] programs; fire prevention, law enforcement, and public information & education; resource protection; and emergency response. 

[5:1:66,148] CAL FIRE limits the damage caused by fires, disasters, environmental degradation, and related emergencies by employing 

[5:2:66,158] diverse yet complementary efforts including: training California's fire service professionals; public education and prevention 

[5:3:66,168] awareness; responsible stewardship of our natural resources; and natural resource and emergency management. 

[6:0:66,186] CAL FIRE's highly trained professionals and leaders cultivate mutually beneficial and cost-effective relationships with all levels 

[6:1:66,196] of government and the private sector to enhance the efficiency and effectiveness of public safety programs and mitigate 

[6:2:66,206] emergencies in the urban, rural, and wild land areas of California. 

[7:0:66,224] CAL FIRE promotes a culture of innovation, accountability, integrity, diversity, planning, workplace safety, and teamwork to 

[7:1:66,234] foster an efficient and effective organizational environment. 

[8:0:66,252] Because CAL FIRE's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to 

[8:1:66,262] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[15:0:66,439] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[16:0:65,457] •   Helicopter Procurement—The Budget includes $101.2 million General Fund to support replacement of the CAL FIRE’s fleet 

[16:1:76,467] of Vietnam War-era helicopters. This funding will begin a multi-year effort to fully replace the Department’s aging fleet with 

[16:2:76,477] new modern helicopters. These new helicopters will have enhanced firefighting capabilities, including the ability to fly at 

[16:3:76,488] night, which will improve CAL FIRE's ability to respond to the increasing number and severity of wildfires resulting from 

[16:4:76,498] climate change. 

[17:0:65,514] •   Climate Change Fire Severity—The Budget includes $10.9 million General Fund to provide heavy equipment mechanics, 

[17:1:76,524] vehicle maintenance funding, and associated administrative support staff to address the nearly year-round fire season. 

[17:2:76,534] These resources build on the funding provided for additional year-round fire engines and extended fire season staffing in the 

[17:3:76,545] 2017 Budget Act and reflect the Administration’s continued efforts to meet the demands of longer, more severe fire seasons 

[17:4:76,555] driven by climate change. 

[18:0:65,571] •   McClellan Reload Base—The Budget includes $4 million General Fund for six permanent positions and 6.1 seasonal 

[18:1:76,581] positions to staff and operate the McClellan Reload Base for the rapid deployment of large air tankers and the California 

[18:2:76,591] National Guard Modular Airborne Fire Fighting Systems. 

[19:0:65,608] •   Fire Training Center—The Budget includes $26.6 million General Fund for CAL FIRE, the California Department of 

[19:1:76,618] Corrections and Rehabilitation, and the California Conservation Corps to operate the Firefighter Training and Certification 

[19:2:76,628] Program for ex-offenders.   The 18-month program will enable 80 ex-offenders, as well as 20 corpsmembers,   to gain the 

[19:3:76,638] training and experience needed to qualify for entry-level firefighting positions with local, state, and federal firefighting 

[19:4:76,648] agencies. 

[20:0:65,665] •   Healthy and Resilient Forests—The Budget includes $160 million Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund and 19 positions to 

[20:1:76,675] support forest health programs that will reduce emissions from wildfires and tree mortality and increase carbon stored in 

[20:2:76,685] living trees in order to maintain California's forests and meet 2030 carbon goals. 

[21:0:65,701] •   Prescribed Fire and Fuel Reduction—The Budget includes $30.8 million to implement recommendations of the 

[21:1:76,711] Administration's California Forest Carbon Plan.   This includes $30 million Greenhouse Gas Reduction Fund and 79 positions 

[22:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[7:0:66,269] 2461 - STATE FIRE MARSHAL 

[8:0:66,287] The Office of the State Fire Marshal is responsible for the statewide protection of life and property through the development 

[8:1:66,297] and application of fire protection engineering, education and enforcement. 

[9:0:66,315] 2461010 - Office of the State Fire Marshal (OSFM) includes the following activities: 

[10:0:65,332] •   Code Development and Analysis: Objectives include the development and adoption of codes relating to fire/life safety used 

[10:1:76,342] statewide by local fire and building authorities. The Code Development and Analysis Division fosters, promotes and develops 

[10:2:76,353] ways and means of protecting life and property against fire in many ways, including the adoption and implementation of 

[10:3:76,363] regulations for statewide application. This division prepares the OSFM fire and life safety regulations and building standards 

[10:4:76,373] for review and adoption into the California Code of Regulations, Titles 19 and 24. The division assists with the application of 

[10:5:76,383] state laws, regulations, and code enforcement relating by a city, county, fire departments or fire districts, and building 

[10:6:76,393] departments. This division also reviews all of California's regulations relating to fire and life safety for relevancy, necessity, 

[10:7:76,404] conflict, duplication and/or overlap. 

[11:0:65,420] •   Fire and Life Safety: This program is the main entity within the department for the application of laws and regulations related 

[11:1:76,430] to fire prevention and life safety. This is achieved through code compliance inspections of new and existing buildings and 

[11:2:76,440] plan review and construction inspections of state-owned and state-occupied projects. The OSFM is responsible for fire & life 

[11:3:76,450] safety in over 45,000 state-owned and state-occupied facilities, which include: state prisons, conservation camps, community 

[11:4:76,461] correctional facilities, state mental hospitals, state developmental centers, California State University and University of 

[11:5:76,471] California campuses, and California Agricultural Districts. 

[12:0:65,487] •   Fire Engineering and Arson and Bomb: The Fire Engineering Division administers licensing programs and provides services 

[12:1:76,497] for product evaluation, certifications and listings. This division also oversees the Arson and Bomb Unit, which has the 

[12:2:76,507] responsibility to dispose seized illegal fireworks and conduct fire/explosion investigations and licensing enforcement. The 

[12:3:76,518] division is responsible for 11 statewide programs, which include the Building Materials Listing; Portable Fire Extinguishers; 

[12:4:76,528] Flame Retardant Chemicals and Fabrics; Vapor Recovery; Automatic Fire Extinguishing Systems; Fire Safe Cigarettes; 

[12:5:76,538] Licensing Enforcement and Inspection; Lab Accreditation; Fireworks; and Motion Picture and Entertainment, Arson and 

[12:6:76,548] Bomb Investigation and Fireworks Disposal. This division's primary functions are licensing, product approval, fire/explosion 

[12:7:76,558] investigations and licensing enforcement. 

[13:0:65,575] •   Pipeline Safety: This program regulates approximately 4,800 miles of intrastate hazardous liquid pipelines that transport 

[13:1:76,585] crude oil and refined products (e.g., gasoline, diesel, jet fuel, etc.) between offshore platforms, production fields, refineries 

[13:2:76,595] and marine terminals. Regulated pipelines do not include those within production fields, marine terminals or refineries. 

[14:0:65,611] •   State Fire Training: This program administers the California Fire Service Training and Education System and the Fire Service 

[14:1:76,621] Training and Education Program for federal, state and local firefighters. The program oversees a California Fire Academy 

[14:2:76,632] System for over 50 training academies that represent a partnership with the fire departments, community colleges and the 

[14:3:76,642] OSFM. State Fire Training administers a professional certification system for firefighters and chief officers and receives 

[14:4:76,652] program guidance from the State Board of Fire Services. 

[15:0:66,670] 2461019 - California Underground Facilities Safe Excavation Board: 

[16:0:66,687] The Board, created by the passage of Chapter 809, Statutes of 2016 (SB 661), works on issues related to the State's "Call 

[16:1:66,698] Before You Dig" law, which requires excavators, except in the case of an emergency, to contact a regional notification center, 

[16:2:66,708] as specified.

[2:0:129,38] 3540   Department of Forestry and Fire Protection - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] 2465 - FIRE PROTECTION 

[4:0:66,83] CAL FIRE provides fire protection to keep damages to life, property and natural resources within social, political and economic 

[4:1:66,93] constraints. The objective is to quickly and aggressively attack all fires in areas where CAL FIRE has assumed primary direct 

[4:2:66,103] protection responsibility by virtue of law, contract or agreement until the fire is under control. The level of initial attack and 

[4:3:66,113] follow-up action is relative to values threatened and control difficulty with the goal to control all unwanted fires within the first 

[4:4:66,123] burning period. 

[5:0:66,141] 2465010 - Fire Prevention: 

[6:0:66,159] This program is responsible for implementation of the Strategic Fire Plan, fire engineering, law enforcement, fire suppression 

[6:1:66,169] cost recovery and fire prevention education. This program focuses on the most effective methods, materials and procedures to 

[6:2:66,179] mitigate hazard and to enforce pertinent laws that deter and mitigate fires. More specifically, efforts focus on what needs to be 

[6:3:66,189] done before a wildland fire starts in order to reduce the costs of fire fighting, property loss, injury to the public and fire fighters 

[6:4:66,200] and damage to the environment. 

[7:0:66,217] 2465019 - Fire Control: 

[8:0:66,235] The objective of this program is to detect, respond and suppress wildland fires in or threatening the State Responsibility Area. 

[8:1:66,245] The heart of the effort is an aggressive initial attack strategy. CAL FIRE's goal is to contain 95 percent of all wildfires to 10 

[8:2:66,255] acres or less. This is achieved through detection, ground attack, air attack and mutual aid using fire engines, fire crews, 

[8:3:66,266] bulldozers, helicopters and fixed wing aircraft. 

[9:0:66,283] 2465028 - Cooperative Fire Protection: 

[10:0:66,301] CAL FIRE provides a full range of fire protection services to local, county, state and federal agencies throughout California 

[10:1:66,311] through the administration of over 100 cooperative government agreements. Additionally, there are six long standing 

[10:2:66,321] agreements wherein counties provide wildland fire protection on behalf of the Department. 

[11:0:66,339] 2465037 - Conservation Camps: 

[12:0:66,357] CAL FIRE, in cooperation with the Department of Corrections and Rehabilitation, Divisions of Adult Operations and Juvenile 

[12:1:66,367] Justice, operates 39 conservation camps throughout the state that house 196 fire crews. These crews provide one of the 

[12:2:66,377] primary labor forces for firefighting, emergency response, and conservation related work projects. 

[13:0:66,395] 2465046 - Emergency Fire Suppression: 

[14:0:66,413] CAL FIRE incurs additional emergency fire suppression expenditures when budgeted initial attack forces are unable to cope 

[14:1:66,423] with a wildland fire within the initial attack period. Portions of these costs may be reimbursed in the fiscal year the expense was 

[14:2:66,433] incurred or in a future fiscal year via Fire Management Assistance Grants provided by the Federal Emergency Management 

[14:3:66,443] Agency (FEMA) or through cost shares provided by other government entities. In addition, CAL FIRE may recover portions of 

[14:4:66,453] this cost through civil cost recovery actions authorized by state statutes. 

[15:0:66,471] 2470 - RESOURCE MANAGEMENT 

[16:0:66,489] California's state and private forest, range, watershed lands and urban forests provide multiple human, climate and 

[16:1:66,499] environmental benefits. The objective of this program is to maintain and enhance those benefits and to minimize damage to 

[16:2:66,509] these resources from natural catastrophes and human development. Objectives are met by regulation of timber harvesting, 

[16:3:66,519] coordination of climate related activities for the forest sector, technical assistance to non-industrial landowners, operation of 

[16:4:66,530] state demonstration forests, operation of forest nurseries, vegetation management projects and administration of federal 

[16:5:66,540] forestry assistance programs. 

[17:0:66,557] 2470010 - Resources Protection and Improvement: 

[18:0:66,575] The objective of this program is to improve forest lands and urban forests. Activities include the detection, evaluation and 

[18:1:66,585] control of forest pests; growing and selling tree seedlings for reforestation, carbon sequestration, and soil erosion control; 

[18:2:66,596] maintenance of a native conifer seed bank; advice and assistance to non-industrial forest landowners on management of 

[18:3:66,606] forests and improved harvesting and conservation practices; research on and demonstration of sustainable forestry in state 

[18:4:66,616] forests; implementation of the California Forest Improvement Act of 1978; technical assistance and grants to foster the 

[18:5:66,626] environmental, social, and economic benefits of urban forests consistent with the California Urban Forestry Act of 1978; and 

[18:6:66,636] purchase and monitoring of working forest conservation easements per the California Forest Legacy Act of 2008. In the area of 

[18:7:66,647] vegetation management, assistance is provided to public and private landowners to achieve land use objectives by reducing 

[18:8:66,657] damage from wildland fires, increase wildlife habitat, increase productivity of forest and rangelands, improve water yields and 

[18:9:66,667] air quality, reduce carbon emissions from wildland fires, increase firefighter safety, and maintain desirable ecosystems. CAL 

[18:10:66,677] FIRE cooperates with federal, state and local agencies, local organizations, and private property owners to develop and 

[18:11:66,687] achieve land use objectives. Activities include removal, rearrangement, conversion, or improvement of vegetation using various 

[18:12:66,698] treatment measures such as prescribed fire and mechanical, manual, and biological methods. The Environmental Protection 

[19:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[3:0:66,65] Program provides in-house expertise and oversight of the Department's compliance with the California Environmental Quality

[3:2:66,93] 2470019 - Forest Practice Regulations: 

[4:0:66,110] The objective of this program is to maximize sustained production of high-quality timber products while protecting soil, water, 

[4:1:66,121] wildlife, recreation and other values associated with forest land. Forest Practice activities include the review and permitting of 

[4:2:66,131] commercial timber harvesting operations on nonfederal timberlands, enforcing state forest practice laws and regulations, and 

[4:3:66,141] monitoring compliance and the effects of timber operations on forest productivity, water quality, watersheds, soils, botanical 

[4:4:66,151] resources, wildlife, fisheries, greenhouse gases and other environmentally sensitive resources. The Program is also 

[4:5:66,161] responsible for issuing licenses to timber operators (LTOs), providing LTO training and education, and assisting the Board of 

[4:6:66,172] Equalization-Timber Tax Division in their administration of taxes on timber and timberland. Forest Practice Program staff also 

[4:7:66,182] assist the Board of Forestry and Fire Protection, and facilitate and participate in monitoring the effectiveness of the Board's 

[4:8:66,192] forest practice regulations. 

[4:9:66,210] 2470028 - Forest Resource Inventory and Assessment: 

[5:0:66,227] The objective of this program is to perform a periodic assessment of California's forest and rangeland resources. Activities 

[5:1:66,238] include maintaining and distributing mission-critical datasets for fire planning, emergency incident support and meeting certain 

[5:2:66,248] regulatory requirements. In addition, the program provides extensive technical and public information for statewide fire threat, 

[5:3:66,258] fire hazard, watersheds, socio-economic conditions, environmental indicators and urban forestry. 

[5:4:66,276] 2470037 - Professional Foresters Registration Program: 

[6:0:66,293] The Office of Professional Foresters Registration is a statutorily authorized program within the State Board of Forestry and Fire 

[6:1:66,304] Protection. The program is responsible for the licensing and regulation of all persons who practice forestry or rangeland 

[6:2:66,314] management on non-federal, state and private lands in California pursuant to the Professional Foresters Law of 1972. 

[6:3:66,332] 2475 - BOARD OF FORESTRY AND FIRE PROTECTION 

[7:0:66,349] The California State Board of Forestry and Fire Protection (Board) is a Governor-appointed body within CAL FIRE. The Board 

[7:1:66,359] is responsible for developing the general forest policy of the state, for determining the guidance policies of CAL FIRE, and for 

[7:2:66,370] representing the state's interest in Federal land in California. Together, the Board and CAL FIRE work to carry out the California 

[7:3:66,380] Legislature's mandate to protect and enhance the State's unique forest and wildland resources. The Board's statutory 

[7:4:66,390] responsibilities are: 

[8:0:65,408] •   Establish and administer forest and rangeland policy for the State of California; 

[8:1:65,424] •   Protect and represent the state's interest in all forestry and rangeland matters; 

[8:2:65,440] •   Provide direction and guidance to the Director and CAL FIRE on fire protection and resource management; 

[8:3:65,456] •   Deliver a comprehensive regulatory program for forestry and fire protection; 

[8:4:65,473] •   Conduct its duties to inform and respond to the people of California. 

[8:5:66,490] 2480 - DEPARTMENT OF JUSTICE LEGAL SERVICES 

[9:0:66,508] The objective of the Department of Justice Legal Services program is to account for the cost of legal services provided by the 

[9:1:66,518] Department of Justice to the California Department of Forestry and Fire Protection. 

[9:2:66,536] 9900100 - ADMINISTRATION 

[10:0:66,554] Departmental administration provides executive leadership, policy direction and a variety of program support services 

[10:1:66,564] necessary for the successful completion of CAL FIRE's mission. CAL FIRE headquarters provides leadership through the 

[10:2:66,574] executive office and through central services in accounting, budgeting, business services, legal, human resources, information 

[10:3:66,584] technology, program accountability and program and systems analysis. CAL FIRE field units provide localized general support

[11:0:66,491] The Department of Forestry and Fire Protection operates more than 530 facilities statewide, including 234 forest fire stations, 

[11:1:66,502] 112 telecommunications facilities, 39 conservation camps, 21 unit headquarters, 16 administrative headquarters, 12 air attack 

[11:2:66,512] bases, 10 helitack bases, 8 state forests, 3 nurseries, a training academy, and various other facilities. These facilities support 

[11:3:66,522] fire protection, the Office of the State Fire Marshal, and resource management efforts for more than 31 million acres of state 

[11:4:66,532] and privately-owned wildlands throughout the state.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   90   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:215,43] 3560   State Lands Commission 

[2:0:66,64] The California State Lands Commission serves the people of California by providing stewardship of the lands, waterways, and 

[2:1:66,74] resources entrusted to its care through preservation, restoration, enhancement, responsible economic development, and the 

[2:2:66,84] promotion of public access. Diligent execution of the Commission's responsibilities since its inception in 1938 has resulted in 

[2:3:66,95] the generation of over $11 billion in revenues while protecting and enhancing the public's ability to enjoy those lands and 

[2:4:66,105] resources. 

[3:0:66,125] Consisting of the Lieutenant Governor, the State Controller, and the Governor's Director of Finance, the California State Lands 

[3:1:66,135] Commission serves as a trustee for the people of the state, managing California's sovereign public trust lands and resources, 

[3:2:66,146] which the state received upon admission into the Union in 1850. It also manages other lands subsequently conveyed to the 

[3:3:66,156] state by the federal government (commonly known as "school" lands) and oversees the management of public trust lands 

[4:0:66,166] legislatively granted in trust to over 70 local jurisdictions. These grants encourage development and use of the state's tidelands 

[4:1:66,176] consistent with the public trust doctrine, and typically require grantees to reinvest revenues produced from the granted lands 

[4:2:66,186] back into the trust. 

[5:0:66,207] Public trust or "sovereign" lands include the beds of all natural and navigable waterways, including non-tidal rivers, streams and 

[5:1:66,217] lakes, and tide and submerged lands within tidal rivers, sloughs, bays and the Pacific Ocean extending from the mean high tide 

[5:2:66,227] line seaward to the three-mile offshore limit and totaling over four and one-half million acres. In addition to sovereign lands, the 

[5:3:66,237] Commission manages "school" lands, which were granted to California by the federal government under the Act of March 3, 

[5:4:66,248] 1853 (10 Stat. 244) for the purpose of supporting public education in California. School lands include the 16th and 36th 

[6:0:66,258] sections of each township (with the exception of lands already reserved for public use or previously taken by private claims) 

[6:1:66,268] and lands known to be mineral in character. Of the five and one-half million acres of school lands originally granted to the state, 

[6:2:66,278] only about 460,370 acres remain in state ownership and these are mostly concentrated in the California desert. The 

[6:3:66,288] Commission also retains a reserved mineral interest in approximately 790,000 acres of sold school lands.

[10:0:66,559] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[10:1:66,577] 2560 - MINERAL RESOURCES MANAGEMENT 

[11:0:66,595] The State Lands Commission oversees efficient and safe development of mineral resources that are located on state lands. 

[11:1:66,605] The State also has a beneficial interest in the Long Beach tidelands oils fields, and as such, the Commission participates in the 

[11:2:66,615] safety and fiscal oversight of these fields. The program objectives are to: manage the responsible extraction of oil, gas, 

[11:3:66,626] geothermal resources and other minerals; collect revenues generated consistent with the best interests of the state, and ensure 

[11:4:66,636] that public safety and environmental protection are maintained at the highest possible standards in the development of these 

[11:5:66,646] resources through engineering review, marine facility inspections, and safety audits. The Commission also adopts regulations 

[11:6:66,656] for the prevention of oil spills, the safe operation of offshore facilities and program and financial audits to ensure the state 

[11:7:66,666] receives fair value for the development of its resources. 

[11:8:66,684] 2565 - LAND MANAGEMENT 

[12:0:66,702] The State Lands Commission manages all ungranted state sovereign lands consistent with the common law public trust 

[12:1:66,712] doctrine and prudent land use and management practices. The Commission issues leases and permits for the use and 

[13:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   92 

[1:0:454,24] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[2:0:180,38] 3560   State Lands Commission - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] occupation of public trust lands under its jurisdiction based upon environmental, economic, health, safety and public benefit 

[3:1:66,75] considerations. The program also manages all state school lands to ensure the greatest economic return to the State Teachers' 

[3:2:66,85] Retirement System. The Commission also oversees the administration of public trust lands legislatively granted to local 

[3:3:66,95] jurisdictions to manage on behalf of the State, including the major ports in the State. 

[3:4:66,113] 2570 - MARINE ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROGRAM 

[4:0:66,131] The State Lands Commission adopts rules, regulations, and guidelines for marine oil terminals within California, providing the 

[4:1:66,141] best achievable protection of the public health and safety and the environment. The Commission's Marine Environmental 

[4:2:66,151] Protection Program monitors the transfer of oil at marine terminals, inspects all marine facilities and reviews, and approves all 

[4:3:66,161] marine oil terminal operations manuals and training and certification programs . The Program also administers Chapter 31F of 

[4:4:66,172] the California Building Code to ensure that marine oil terminals are fit for purpose. In addition, the Marine Environmental 

[4:5:66,182] Protection Program implements the state's Marine Invasive Species Program to prevent the introduction of non-indigenous 

[4:6:66,192] species into California waters through vessel mediated vectors. The program funds and conducts scientific research, develops 

[4:7:66,202] rules and regulations to implement the Marine Invasive Species Act, and physically inspects oceangoing vessels to assess 

[4:8:66,212] compliance with vessel-reported nonindigenous species management actions. 

[4:9:66,230] 9900100 - EXECUTIVE AND ADMINISTRATION 

[5:0:66,248] The Executive and Administration program provides management, policy direction and administrative support to the line 

[5:1:66,258] programs of the Commission.

[1:0:197,43] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Department of Fish and Wildlife is to manage California's diverse fish, wildlife, and plant resources, and the 

[2:1:66,74] habitats upon which they depend, for their ecological values and for their use and enjoyment by the public. 

[3:0:66,92] This includes habitat protection and maintenance in a sufficient amount and quality to ensure the survival of all species and 

[3:1:66,102] natural communities. The Department is also responsible for the diversified use of fish and wildlife including recreational, 

[3:2:66,112] commercial, scientific, and educational uses. 

[4:0:66,130] Because the Department of Fish and Wildlife programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital 

[4:1:66,140] outlay program to support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure 

[4:2:66,150] Overview."

[20:0:66,605] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[21:0:65,623] •   Sustainable Funding for Fish and Wildlife—$31.6 million ($26.6 million General Fund and $5 million California Tire Recycling 

[21:1:76,633] Management Fund) to (1) continue the current level of service for core fish and wildlife program; (2) augment high-priority

[2:0:162,38] 3600   Department of Fish and Wildlife - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] This program facilitates diverse and sustainable hunting, fishing (recreational and commercial), trapping, and other public uses 

[3:1:66,75] and associated economic benefits to the state by conserving and managing game species. Activities include collection and 

[3:2:66,85] assessment of information on the distribution and abundance of game fish and wildlife to determine appropriate regulations 

[3:3:66,95] (bag limits, gear restrictions, etc.) and to monitor the effects of those regulations. 

[3:4:66,113] 2600 - MANAGEMENT OF DEPARTMENT LANDS AND FACILITIES PROGRAM 

[4:0:66,131] This program manages Department-owned or leased lands and facilities, including hatcheries, wildlife areas, ecological 

[4:1:66,141] reserves, fish and wildlife laboratories, and public access areas, to contribute to the conservation, protection, and management 

[4:2:66,151] of fish and wildlife. 

[4:3:66,169] 2605 - ENFORCEMENT 

[5:0:66,187] This program serves the public through law enforcement, public safety and hunter education. Law enforcement promotes 

[5:1:66,197] compliance with laws and regulations protecting fish and wildlife resources; investigates habitat destruction, pollution incidents 

[5:2:66,207] and illegal commercialization of wildlife. Wardens also serve the public through general law enforcement, mutual aid and 

[5:3:66,217] homeland security. 

[6:0:66,235] 2610 - COMMUNICATIONS, EDUCATION, AND OUTREACH PROGRAM 

[7:0:66,253] This program serves the public through resource conservation education and use activities in the classroom and on public and 

[7:1:66,263] private lands, community and stakeholder outreach, and the delivery of information and data using a variety of methods 

[7:2:66,273] including publications, presentations, web applications and media relations. 

[7:3:66,291] 2615 - SPILL PREVENTION AND RESPONSE PROGRAM 

[8:0:66,308] This program prevents damage, minimizes environmental impacts, restores, and rehabilitates California's fish and wildlife 

[8:1:66,319] populations and their habitats from the harmful effects of oil and other deleterious material spills in marine waters and inland 

[8:2:66,329] habitats. 

[9:0:66,347] 2620 - FISH AND GAME COMMISSION 

[10:0:66,364] The California Fish and Game Commission ensures the long term sustainability of California's fish and wildlife resources by 

[10:1:66,374] guiding the ongoing scientific evaluation and assessment of California's fish and wildlife resources; setting California's fish and 

[10:2:66,385] wildlife resource management policies and ensuring these are implemented by the Department of Fish and Wildlife; 

[10:3:66,395] establishing appropriate fish and wildlife resource management rules and regulations; and building active fish and wildlife 

[10:4:66,405] resource management partnerships with individual landowners, the public and interest groups, and federal, state and local 

[10:5:66,415] resource management agencies.

[6:0:66,335] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[7:0:66,353] The Department of Fish and Wildlife manages 742 properties statewide, comprising more than 1 million acres (approximately 

[7:1:66,363] 679,000 acres of state-owned land and 484,000 acres owned by other entities, but managed by the Department. Several state 

[7:2:66,373] agencies purchase land for the purpose of habitat or wildlife protection, and management responsibilities for these properties 

[7:3:66,383] are often transferred to the Department; therefore, the amount of land under Department control continues to increase. The 

[7:4:66,394] properties managed by the Department include: 111 wildlife areas, 136 ecological reserves, 138 public access areas, and 20 

[7:5:66,404] fish hatcheries.

[7:0:66,398] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[7:1:66,416] 2710 - WILDLIFE CONSERVATION BOARD 

[8:0:66,434] The Wildlife Conservation Board's three main functions are (1) land acquisition, (2) habitat protection, restoration, and 

[8:1:66,444] enhancement, including conservation of inland wetlands, riparian habitat, oak woodlands and protection of rangeland, grazing 

[8:2:66,454] land and grasslands, and (3) development of wildlife-oriented public access and recreational areas.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   132   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:198,43] 3720   California Coastal Commission 

[2:0:66,64] The California Coastal Commission, comprised of 12 voting members appointed equally by the Governor, the Senate Rules 

[2:1:66,74] Committee, and the Speaker of the Assembly, was created by voter initiative in 1972 and was made permanent by the 

[2:2:66,84] California Coastal Act of 1976 (Coastal Act). The Coastal Act calls for the protection and enhancement of public access and 

[2:3:66,95] recreation, marine resources, environmentally sensitive habitat areas, marine water quality, agriculture, and scenic resources, 

[2:4:66,105] and makes provisions for coastal-dependent industrial and energy development. New development in the coastal zone requires 

[2:5:66,115] a coastal permit either from local government or the Commission. Local governments are required to prepare a local coastal 

[2:6:66,125] program (LCP) for the coastal zone portion of their jurisdiction. After an LCP has been reviewed and approved by the 

[2:7:66,135] Commission as being consistent with the Coastal Act, the Commission's regulatory authority over most types of new 

[2:8:66,146] development is delegated to the local government, subject to limited appeals to the Commission. The Commission also is 

[2:9:66,156] designated the principal state coastal management agency for the purpose of administering the federal Coastal Zone 

[2:10:66,166] Management Act in California and has exclusive regulatory authority over federal activities such as permits, leases, federal 

[2:11:66,176] development projects, and other federal actions that could affect coastal zone resources and that would not otherwise be 

[2:12:66,186] subject to state control.

[5:1:66,271] 2730 - COASTAL MANAGEMENT PROGRAM 

[6:0:66,289] The objectives of the Coastal Management Program are to implement coastal resources conservation through planning and 

[6:1:66,299] regulation. Activities include: 

[7:0:65,317] •   Reviewing and approval of local coastal programs (LCPs), port master plans, university long-range development plans, and 

[7:1:76,327] any amendments to such plans, for consistency with the Coastal Act. 

[7:2:65,343] •   Reviewing coastal development permit applications for new development in areas without a certified LCP, areas of 

[7:3:76,354] permanently retained jurisdiction (e.g., tidelands, submerged lands, and public trust lands) and limited categories of local 

[7:4:76,364] coastal development permit actions that can be appealed to the Commission. 

[7:5:65,380] •   Monitoring and enforcement of coastal development permits. 

[7:6:65,396] •   Reviewing federal activities for consistency with the Coastal Act. 

[7:7:65,412] •   Protecting and expanding opportunities for public coastal access and recreation. 

[7:8:65,429] •   Implementing a coastal water quality protection program. 

[7:9:65,445] •   Providing technical information and assistance to support effective coastal management. 

[7:10:65,461] •   Implementing a coastal and ocean resource public education program. 

[7:11:66,479] 2735 - COASTAL ENERGY PROGRAM 

[8:0:66,496] The Coastal Energy Program addresses coastal energy issues including, but not limited to, offshore oil and gas development, 

[8:1:66,507] electricity generating power plant expansion and development, and siting and development of liquefied natural gas facilities. 

[8:2:66,524] 2736 - ADMINISTRATIVE SUPPORT 

[9:0:66,542] The Administration Support objective is to provide administrative support including accounting, budgeting, business services, 

[9:1:66,552] support services, information technology, and human resources services to other state departmental programs.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   139 

[1:0:210,43] 3760   State Coastal Conservancy 

[2:0:66,64] The State Coastal Conservancy develops and implements projects to protect, restore and enhance natural, recreational, and 

[2:1:66,74] economic resources along California's coast, coastal watersheds, the ocean, and within the San Francisco Bay Area. The 

[3:0:66,84] Coastal Conservancy supports implementation of the San Francisco Bay Area Conservancy Program and the Santa Ana River 

[3:1:66,95] Conservancy Program. Through technical assistance, direct funding and grants to local agencies and nonprofit organizations, 

[3:2:66,105] the Coastal Conservancy supports projects that: 

[4:0:65,123] •   Protect and enhance the natural environment, agricultural lands, scenic lands and urban waterfronts along the Coast and 

[4:1:76,133] around San Francisco Bay. 

[5:0:65,149] •   Increase public access to the coast and San Francisco Bay 

[5:1:65,165] •   Build trails and increase outdoor recreational opportunities. 

[5:2:65,181] •   Enhance coastal wetlands and other important habitat areas. 

[6:0:65,198] •   Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area. 

[6:1:65,214] •   Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12. 

[7:0:66,244] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS 

[8:0:339,265] Positions   Expenditures 

[8:1:304,279] 2016-17   2017-18   2018-19   2016-17*   2017-18*   2018-19*

[4:0:66,100] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[4:1:66,119] 2790 - COASTAL CONSERVANCY PROGRAM 

[4:2:66,136] The Coastal Conservancy seeks to achieve the following goals: 

[4:3:65,154] •   Protect and enhance habitats, wetlands, scenic open space, working lands and urban waterfronts along the California coast 

[4:4:76,164] and around San Francisco Bay. 

[5:0:65,180] •   Increase public access to the coast and outdoor recreational opportunities by creating new parks, trails and recreational 

[5:1:76,191] amenities. 

[6:0:65,207] •   Prepare for and reduce the unavoidable impacts of climate change on the coastal region and San Francisco Bay Area. 

[6:1:65,223] •   Support education programs about coastal resources for kindergarten through grade 12. 

[6:2:65,239] •   Facilitate improvement of existing, and the development of new, lower cost accommodations within one and one-half miles of 

[6:3:76,249] the coast. 

[6:4:66,267] 2805 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY LOCAL ASSISTANCE PROGRAM 

[6:5:66,285] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to local governments, nonprofits and other project 

[6:6:66,295] partners for the acquisition of land and easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State 

[6:7:66,305] Coastal Conservancy helps to implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program and supports programs 

[6:8:66,315] that increase public access to the coast. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of more than 390,000 acres of 

[6:9:66,326] coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has helped restore more than 

[6:10:66,336] 33,000 acres of habitat. 

[7:0:66,354] Through its projects, the Conservancy has established approximately 160 new coastal accessways and built more than 168 

[7:1:66,364] miles of new trails. 

[8:0:66,381] State Coastal Conservancy funds are primarily expended for purposes of local assistance but may also be expended for capital 

[8:1:66,392] outlay purposes, see Program 2820. 

[8:2:66,409] 2820 - STATE COASTAL CONSERVANCY CAPITAL OUTLAY PROGRAM 

[8:3:66,427] The State Coastal Conservancy provides grants and technical assistance to state agencies for the acquisition of land and 

[8:4:66,437] easements, the development of public facilities, and the restoration of habitat. The State Coastal Conservancy helps to 

[8:5:66,447] implement the State's federally approved Coastal Management Program. The Conservancy has participated in the protection of 

[8:6:66,458] more than 390,000 acres of coastal lands through acquisition of fee title and conservation easements. The Conservancy has 

[8:7:66,468] helped restore more than 33,000 acres of habitat.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   154   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:183,43] 3790   Department of Parks and Recreation 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the California Department of Parks and Recreation (Parks) is to provide for the health, inspiration, and 

[3:0:66,74] education of the people of California by helping to preserve the state's extraordinary biological diversity, protecting its most 

[3:1:66,84] valued natural, cultural and historical resources, and creating opportunities for high-quality outdoor recreation for current and 

[3:2:66,95] future generations to enjoy. With increased urbanization, the establishment of park units and recreation areas accessible to the 

[3:3:66,105] major population centers of the state has become particularly important. Specific activities include: stewardship of natural 

[3:4:66,115] resources, historic, cultural and archeological sites, and artifacts and structures; provision of interpretive services for park 

[3:5:66,125] visitors; construction and maintenance of campsites, trails, visitor centers, museums, and infrastructure such as roads and 

[3:6:66,135] water systems; and creation of recreational opportunities such as hiking, bicycling, fishing, swimming, horseback riding, 

[3:7:66,146] jogging, camping, picnicking, and off-highway vehicle recreation. In addition, the Division of Boating and Waterways funds, 

[3:8:66,156] plans, and develops boating facilities on waterways throughout California and ensures safe boating for the public by providing 

[3:9:66,166] financial aid and training to local law enforcement agencies. 

[4:0:66,184] Because Parks' program drives a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to support 

[4:1:66,194] this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview."

[2:0:148,38] 3790   Department of Parks and Recreation - Continued
