[4:0:66,126] LEGAL CITATIONS AND AUTHORITY 

[5:0:66,144] DEPARTMENT AUTHORITY 

[6:0:66,162] Public Resources Code, Division 1, Chapter 1 et seq. and Public Resources Code, Division 5, Chapter 1 et seq. 

[7:0:66,199] MAJOR PROGRAM CHANGES 

[8:0:65,217] •   Fixing State Parks and Improving Access for All Californians—$42 million State Parks and Recreation Fund and 364 

[8:1:76,227] positions to increase services that emphasize those that directly benefit park visitors by improving access, and fixing and 

[8:2:76,237] maintaining the aging infrastructure of the state parks system. The largest area of investment is facilities and maintenance, 

[8:3:76,247] which will contribute to maintaining clean water supplies, clean restrooms, dynamic trail systems, stable historic structures, 

[8:4:76,258] and safe roads. These additional resources will also help address more than $1 billion in deferred maintenance projects 

[8:5:76,268] throughout the state parks system. The natural and cultural management funding will allow the Department to better 

[8:6:76,278] understand, protect, and preserve California's biologically and culturally diverse places under its care. The interpretation and 

[8:7:76,288] education augmentations will build upon the Department's recent focus on developing stronger relationships with the public. 

[8:8:76,298] The public safety positions will focus on providing a safe visitor experience in complex park environments and protecting 

[8:9:76,309] cultural and natural resources. 

[9:0:65,325] •   Solving the Structural Imbalance and Establishing a Reserve—$26.6 million in permanent funding from the State Parks and 

[9:1:76,335] Recreation Fund to address the structural imbalance. Since 2012, the Department has relied on one-time appropriations to 

[9:2:76,345] maintain services across the park system. The Budget ends that pattern and provides sustainable ongoing funding, including 

[9:3:76,355] $8.5 million to establish a prudent reserve balance in the Fund. 

[10:0:65,372] •   Fix Our Parks Investments—$3 million State Parks and Recreation Fund   for the following,   $1 million to make permanent the 

[10:1:76,382] newly established recruitment and training program for hard-to-fill classifications; $1 million for increased law enforcement, 

[10:2:76,392] environmental monitoring, and maintenance grants supporting federal off-highway vehicle recreation; and $1 million for the 

[10:3:76,402] Abandoned Watercraft Abatement grant program to remove abandoned watercraft from California's waterways. 

[11:0:65,418] •   State Parks System Enhancements—$4 million to begin project identification and planning activities necessary to 

[11:1:76,429] strategically allocate Proposition 68 funds dedicated to the state park system. Of this amount, $1.9 million will support 

[11:2:76,439] implementation of the Redwoods Rising project to enhance old growth coastal redwoods, in collaboration with the National 

[11:3:76,449] Park Service and the Save the Redwoods League. 

[12:0:65,465] •   Safe Neighborhood Parks—$277 million to provide grants for the creation of new parks and recreation facilities, as well as 

[12:1:76,475] the rehabilitation of existing local parks, in critically underserved communities throughout California, consistent with the 

[12:2:76,486] Statewide Park Development and Community Revitalization Program. 

[13:0:65,502] •   Per Capita Grants—$186 million to provide for the acquisition and development of neighborhood, community, and regional 

[13:1:76,512] parks and recreation lands and facilities in urban and rural areas. 

[14:0:65,528] •   California Indian Heritage Center—$100 million General Fund for the Department to construct a new museum to protect and 

[14:1:76,538] preserve the history of California’s native peoples. The total cost of the project is estimated at $200 million. The 

[14:2:76,549] Administration will work with stakeholders to raise the additional $100 million necessary to complete the project. This new 

[14:3:76,559] museum would replace the current State Indian Museum, which was built in 1940 on the grounds of Sutter’s Fort State 

[14:4:76,569] Historic Park. The Department would operate the museum and work with tribal representatives in developing appropriate 

[14:5:76,579] content for the interpretive program to engage visitors in celebrating the living cultures of California Tribes. 

[15:0:65,595] •   Fort Ross State Historic Park: Cultural Trail—$852,000 Proposition 12 bond funds   to design a new trail focusing on 

[15:1:76,606] increasing the interpretive program at Fort Ross State Historic Park. The trail would complete a segment of the California 

[15:2:76,616] Coastal Trail and a multimedia interpretive program encouraging park visitors to explore the complex history of the site. The 

[15:3:76,626] interpretive program will be developed through a partnership with stakeholders including the native people of the area and 

[15:4:76,636] UC Berkeley. Total project costs are estimated to be $3.4 million. 

[16:0:65,652] •   Museum of Tolerance Renovation Project—$10 million General Fund on a one-time   basis   for the Department of Parks and 

[16:1:76,663] Recreation to provide a grant for the Museum's renovation project. This funding will provide half of the estimated $20 million 

[16:2:76,673] cost of the renovation to fund critical capital improvements. 

[17:0:65,689] •   Legacy Forests at State Parks—$15 million Proposition 68 for the Department of Parks and Recreation to protect forests in 

[17:1:76,699] state parks. This funding will support restoration efforts in state parks throughout the state, including coastal redwoods, 

[17:2:76,709] central Sierra sequoias, coastal pines, and oak woodlands. 

[18:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   182   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:177,43] 3810   Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy 

[2:0:66,64] The Santa Monica Mountains Conservancy acquires, restores, and consolidates lands in the Santa Monica Mountains, Rim of 

[2:1:66,74] the Valley Trail Corridor, Upper Los Angeles River Watershed, and watersheds of Santa Monica Bay for park, recreation, and 

[2:2:66,84] conservation purposes. The Conservancy may: 

[2:3:65,102] •   Lease, rent, sell, transfer, or exchange lands for park purposes. 

[3:0:65,118] •   Award grants or interest free loans to state and local agencies to purchase or restore park, recreation, conservation, or 

[3:1:76,129] buffer-zone areas to ensure that the character and intensity of development on these lands is generally compatible with and 

[3:2:76,139] does not adversely impact the environment. 

[4:0:65,155] •   Implement programs to improve access from the inner city areas surrounding the Conservancy, thereby providing 

[4:1:76,165] recreational opportunities for all residents. 

[4:2:65,181] •   Manage the Rim of the Valley Trail Corridor recreational trail. 

[5:0:65,198] •   Plans for restoration, recreation, and conservation projects for the Los Angeles River and Upper Los Angeles River 

[5:1:76,208] Watershed.

[1:0:104,43] 3820   San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission 

[2:0:66,64] The San Francisco Bay Conservation and Development Commission is a state planning and regulatory agency with regional 

[2:1:66,74] authority. Its mission is to protect and enhance San Francisco Bay and to encourage the Bay's responsible and productive use 

[2:2:66,84] for this and future generations. The Commission authored and maintains the San Francisco Bay Plan and relies on it, the 

[3:0:66,95] McAteer-Petris Act, and other regulatory authority to maximize public access to the Bay and minimize Bay fill. The Commission 

[3:1:66,105] issues permits for filling, dredging, and development projects within the Bay, along the Bay shoreline, and within salt ponds and 

[3:2:66,115] certain managed wetlands adjacent to the Bay. The Commission also implements the Suisun Marsh Preservation Act of 1977. 

[3:3:66,125] By state statute, the Commission develops and implements the federal Coastal Zone Management Act's program for the Bay 

[3:4:66,135] and exercises authority over federal activities otherwise not subject to state control. The Commission leads the ongoing multi-

[3:5:66,146] agency regional effort to address the impacts of sea level rise and climate change on the Bay and its environs. Funding for 

[3:6:66,156] these efforts to address climate change is derived mainly from federal grants and other agreements, contracts, and 

[3:7:66,166] reimbursements.

[1:0:195,43] 3830   San Joaquin River Conservancy 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the San Joaquin River Conservancy is to acquire, preserve, manage, and promote access to lands within the 

[2:1:66,74] flood plain on both sides of the San Joaquin River from Friant Dam to Highway 99. 

[2:2:66,92] Specific activities are to: 

[3:0:65,110] •   Implement the San Joaquin River Parkway Master Plan, a 22-mile regional greenspace and wildlife corridor along both sides 

[3:1:76,120] of the river extending from Friant Dam to Highway 99, with an interconnected trail system and recreational and educational 

[3:2:76,130] features. 

[4:0:65,146] •   Acquire approximately 5,900 acres from willing sellers. 

[5:0:65,162] •   Operate and manage lands for public enjoyment consistent with the protection of natural resources. 

[5:1:65,179] •   Protect, enhance, and restore riparian and riverine habitat and ecological diversity. 

[5:2:65,195] •   Provide for multi-benefit water quality, water supply, ecosystem and watershed protection and restoration. 

[5:3:65,211] •   Facilitate the development of the parkway, garner public support, and secure its future.

[1:0:210,43] 3835   Baldwin Hills Conservancy 

[1:1:66,64] The Baldwin Hills Conservancy was established in 2000 to acquire open space and manage public lands within the Baldwin 

[1:2:66,74] Hills area and to provide recreation, restoration, and protection of wildlife habitat for the public's enjoyment and educational 

[1:3:66,84] experience. 

[1:4:66,102] Specific agency responsibilities are to: 

[1:5:65,120] •   Approve the Baldwin Hills Park Master Plan. 

[2:0:65,136] •   Undertake site improvement projects, regulate public access, construct new facilities as needed for outdoor recreation, 

[2:1:76,146] nature appreciation and natural resource protection. 

[3:0:65,162] •   Prioritize and implement acquisition of additional recreational and open space land within the Baldwin Hills area. 

[3:1:65,179] •   Provide recreational, open space, wildlife habitat restoration and protection of lands for educational use within the area. 

[3:2:65,195] •   Conduct the necessary planning activities for the area. 

[4:0:65,211] •   Approve conservancy funded projects that advance the policies and priorities set forth by the enabling statute. 

[4:1:65,227] •   Develop and coordinate a program of resource stewardship for optimum recreational and natural resource value based on 

[4:2:76,237] the needs of the surrounding community. 

[5:0:65,254] •   Provide for the public's enjoyment, and enhance recreational and educational experiences on public lands in a manner 

[5:1:76,264] consistent with the protection of lands and resources in the area.

[1:0:203,43] 3840   Delta Protection Commission 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Delta Protection Commission is to protect, maintain, enhance and enrich the overall quality of the Delta 

[2:1:66,74] environment and economy, with a focus on agriculture, recreation and natural resources, and mindful of the importance of the 

[2:2:66,84] Delta to all Californians. The Commission ensures orderly, balanced conservation and development of Delta land resources 

[2:3:66,95] and provides a forum for Delta residents to engage in decisions regarding actions to recognize and enhance the unique 

[2:4:66,105] cultural, recreational, and agricultural resources of the Delta. The Commission identifies and makes recommendations on 

[2:5:66,115] methods of preserving the Delta as an evolving place, and promotes Delta legacy communities, regional economic 

[2:6:66,125] sustainability, and emergency response preparedness.

[1:0:202,43] 3845   San Diego River Conservancy 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the San Diego River Conservancy is to achieve the goals of the San Diego River Conservancy Act by 

[2:1:66,74] implementing five major programs: Land Conservation, Recreation and Education, Preserve Natural/Cultural Resources, Water

[2:3:66,95] building, with our partners, a San Diego River Park and hiking trail stretching from the headwaters in Julian to the Pacific 

[2:4:66,105] Ocean. These programs foster collaboration among governments and nonprofit organizations, and leverage grant funding to

[4:1:66,221] 3220 - SIERRA NEVADA CONSERVANCY PROGRAM 

[4:2:66,239] Established pursuant to Chapter 726, Statutes of 2004, the Conservancy is authorized to: 

[4:3:65,256] •   Provide increased opportunities for tourism and recreation. 

[5:0:65,273] •   Protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, cultural, archaeological, historical, and living resources. 

[5:1:65,289] •   Aid in the preservation of working landscapes. 

[5:2:65,305] •   Reduce the risk of natural disasters, such as wildfires. 

[5:3:65,321] •   Protect and improve water and air quality. 

[6:0:65,337] •   Assist the regional economy through the operation of the Conservancy's program. 

[6:1:65,354] •   Undertake efforts to enhance public use and enjoyment of lands owned by the public. 

[7:0:66,371] In support of these programs, the Conservancy facilitates collaborative planning efforts with local and regional governments, 

[7:1:66,381] community groups and interested parties, provides technical assistance, and initiates and funds projects to identify and fill 

[7:2:66,392] critical needs in the Sierra Nevada region.

[0:0:66,13] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   222   NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:196,43] 3860   Department of Water Resources 

[2:0:66,64] The Department of Water Resources protects, conserves, develops, and manages California's water. The Department 

[3:0:66,74] evaluates existing water resources, forecasts future water needs and explores future potential solutions to meet ever-growing 

[3:1:66,84] needs for personal use, irrigation, industry, recreation, power generation, and fish and wildlife. The Department also works to 

[4:0:66,95] prevent and minimize flood damage, oversee the safety of dams, and educate the public about the importance of water and its 

[4:1:66,105] efficient use. 

[5:0:66,123] Because the Department's programs drive a need for infrastructure investment, the Department has a capital outlay program to 

[5:1:66,133] support this need. For the specifics on the Department's capital outlay program see "Infrastructure Overview." 

[6:0:66,163] 3-YEAR EXPENDITURES AND POSITIONS 

[7:0:318,184] Positions   Expenditures 

[7:1:283,198] 2016-17   2017-18   2018-19   2016-17*   2017-18*   2018-19* 

[7:2:75,210] 3230   Continuing Formulation of the California 

[7:3:115,215] Water Plan   331.2   319.4   352.2   $169,875   $757,136   $338,375 

[7:4:75,232] 3240   Implementation of the State Water 

[7:5:115,237] Resources Development System   1,877.0   1,793.6   1,828.5   779,139   1,679,826   1,696,537

[6:1:65,465] •   Flood Control Infrastructure—$393 million ($295 million General Fund and $98.5 million Proposition 68) to enhance the 

[6:2:76,475] state’s resilience to catastrophic flooding. Funding includes (1) $170 million General Fund to support the state cost-share of 

[6:3:76,485] U.S. Army Corps of Engineers' urban flood control projects, (2) $98.5 million Proposition 68 for flood control projects and 

[6:4:76,495] floodplain enhancements that achieve public safety and fish and wildlife improvements, (3) $100 million General Fund to 

[6:5:76,505] address deferred maintenance projects on levees, and (4) $25 million General Fund to support ongoing levee maintenance, 

[6:6:76,516] including providing incentives for cost-sharing with local maintaining agencies. 

[7:0:65,532] •   Groundwater Sustainability—$61.8 million from Proposition 68 to support groundwater sustainability agencies through three 

[7:1:76,542] key efforts: (1) providing technical assistance to aid in the development and evaluation of their plans, (2) supplementing 

[7:2:76,552] existing planning grants to support a groundwater sustainability agency's responsibility to define a path to achieve 

[7:3:76,562] sustainable groundwater management, and (3) providing grants directly supporting implementation of groundwater projects. 

[7:4:65,579] •   Water Conservation—$300,000 Environmental License Plate Fund to support ongoing outreach and advertising to sustain 

[7:5:76,589] the Save Our Water campaign as an effective tool to promote water conservation as a part of Californians’ everyday lives.

[5:0:66,615] PROGRAM DESCRIPTIONS 

[6:0:66,633] 3230 - CONTINUING FORMULATION OF THE CALIFORNIA WATER PLAN 

[7:0:66,651] The California Water Plan is the state's strategic plan for the efficient use, management and development of the state's water 

[7:1:66,661] resources. The Plan is updated every five years and provides a framework for water managers, legislators, and the public to 

[7:2:66,671] consider options and make decisions regarding California's water future. The Plan evaluates current and future water 

[7:3:66,681] conditions, challenges and opportunities. It presents basic data and information on California's water resources including water 

[7:4:66,692] supply evaluations and assessments of existing and future agricultural, urban, and environmental water uses. The Plan 

[7:5:66,702] identifies and evaluates multiple future scenarios and over 30 resource management strategies such as conservation, 

[7:6:66,712] recycling, desalination, transfers, storage, conveyance, water quality, watershed management, ecosystem restoration, 

[8:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

# === PAGE 227 === [size: 612x792]
[0:0:66,24] NATURAL RESOURCES 

[1:0:408,24] 2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   225 

[2:0:161,38] 3860   Department of Water Resources - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] groundwater, and urban land use management to help meet future demands in light of uncertainties and unexpected 

[3:1:66,75] catastrophic events. This program also identifies ways for the state to: (1) help local agencies and governments prepare 

[3:2:66,85] integrated regional water management plans on a watershed basis and diversify their regional water portfolios to ensure 

[3:3:66,95] sustainable water uses, reliable water supplies, better water quality, environmental stewardship, efficient urban development, 

[3:4:66,106] protection of agriculture, and a strong economy, (2) help cities, counties and local agencies prepare a Water Element for their 

[3:5:66,116] General Plans, Urban Water Management Plans and Agricultural Water Management Plans, and (3) help local agencies and 

[3:6:66,126] tribal governments improve coordination between water and land use planning. 

[4:0:66,144] 3240 - IMPLEMENTATION OF THE STATE WATER RESOURCES DEVELOPMENT SYSTEM 

[5:0:66,161] The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage facilities (reservoirs and lakes), 21 

[5:1:66,172] pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700 miles of conveyance (canals, 

[5:2:66,182] pipelines, and tunnels). The Project provides water to over 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated farmland. 

[6:0:66,200] The Department plans, designs, constructs, operates, maintains, and manages State Water Project facilities which provide 

[6:1:66,210] water to facilities located from Plumas County to Riverside County. 

[7:0:66,227] The California WaterFix Program is charged with improving the Delta ecosystem and ensuring water supply reliability in a safe, 

[7:1:66,238] timely, and cost-effective manner. This includes implementation of the California WaterFix, project specific environmental 

[7:2:66,248] commitments, and compliance with the Environmental Impact Report and Environmental Impact Statement and other state and 

[7:3:66,258] federal regulations and permits. 

[8:0:66,276] 3245 - PUBLIC SAFETY AND PREVENTION OF DAMAGE 

[9:0:66,293] This program supports the California Water Action Plan by protecting life and property from damage by floods, ensures proper 

[9:1:66,304] construction and maintenance of jurisdictional dams and levees, and provides loans for the construction. Activities include 

[9:2:66,314] assessing the state and regional investment needs to reduce risk, preventive floodplain management to discourage unwise use 

[9:3:66,324] of areas subject to flooding, protection of floodplains, issuance of flood warnings, operation of flood control facilities, 

[9:4:66,334] coordination and supervision of flood fight activities, and annual levee and flood channel maintenance and inspection in 

[9:5:66,344] cooperation with other local, state, and federal partner agencies. This program also buys land, easements, and rights-of-way 

[9:6:66,355] for federal flood control projects and supervises the design and construction of new dams and periodic inspection and 

[10:0:66,365] reevaluation of all existing jurisdictional dams for proper operation and maintenance. Fiscal oversight and coordination activities 

[10:1:66,375] associated with the Disaster Preparedness and Flood Prevention Bond Act of 2006 are administered under this program. The 

[10:2:66,385] program also reviews federal dam projects in coordination with federal and other state agencies with regard to dam safety. 

[11:0:66,403] 3250 - CENTRAL VALLEY FLOOD PROTECTION BOARD 

[12:0:66,421] The Central Valley Flood Protection Board has regulatory authority over the State Plan of Flood Control facilities (SPFC), 

[12:1:66,431] designated floodways and regulated streams in the Central Valley. The Board regulates encroachments on the system by 

[12:2:66,441] issuing permits and initiating enforcement action when necessary to maintain the integrity of the levees and floodways that 

[12:3:66,451] protect the valley's people and property. The Board manages the state's portfolio of real property held by the Sacramento-San 

[12:4:66,461] Joaquin Drainage District. The Board serves as the non-federal sponsor to the United States Army Corps of Engineers on large 

[12:5:66,472] joint state-federal levee improvement projects and assists the more than one hundred local maintaining agencies that operate 

[12:6:66,482] and maintain the SPFC. The Board conducts regular public meetings, workshops and tours, providing a public forum for 

[12:7:66,492] stakeholders. 

[13:0:66,510] 3255 - SERVICES 

[14:0:66,527] This program provides technical support within the Department and expertise in the fields of water resources planning, 

[14:1:66,538] development and management; watermaster services; scientific analyses performed by DWR's chemical laboratory; 

[14:2:66,548] information technology; and mapping, surveying and engineering services for other agencies. 

[15:0:66,566] 3260 - CALIFORNIA ENERGY RESOURCES SCHEDULING 

[16:0:66,583] For a limited period of time, this program purchased electric power on behalf of the state's investor-owned utilities. Beginning 

[16:1:66,593] January 1, 2003, the utility companies resumed responsibility for purchasing power from the spot market. The utilities, however, 

[16:2:66,604] continued to receive power from the Department's long-term energy contracts with energy suppliers, under which the 

[16:3:66,614] Department retains legal and financial responsibility. All energy contracts signed by the Department have now expired or were 

[16:4:66,624] terminated. However, litigation continues against some of the counterparties to these contracts. Additionally, the Department 

[16:5:66,634] retains the legal and financial responsibility for administering $4.621 billion in revenue bonds issued to repay the General Fund 

[16:6:66,644] for money borrowed for power purchases during the energy crisis and funding of reserve accounts necessary to maintain an 

[16:7:66,655] investment grade credit rating associated with the revenue bonds. 

[17:0:66,736] * Dollars in thousands, except in Salary Range. Numbers may not add or match to other statements due to rounding of budget details.

[2:0:161,38] 3860   Department of Water Resources - Continued 

[3:0:66,65] INFRASTRUCTURE OVERVIEW 

[4:0:66,83] The Department of Water Resources has two major infrastructure programs: the State Water Project and the flood control 

[4:1:66,93] system in the Central Valley. The State Water Project is a water storage and delivery system that consists of 34 storage 

[4:2:66,104] facilities, reservoirs, and lakes, 21 pumping plants, 4 pumping-generating plants, 5 hydroelectric power plants, and over 700 

[4:3:66,114] miles of canals, pipelines, and tunnels. The Project provides water to 25 million Californians and 750,000 acres of irrigated 

[4:4:66,124] farmland. The Project also provides recreational opportunities to approximately 6 million visitors annually at 15 separate 

[4:5:66,134] reservoir-based facilities as well as public fishing access to much of the Aqueduct. Existing flood control infrastructure in the 

[4:6:66,144] Central Valley consists of 1,595 miles of levees, 348,000 acres of channels and floodways, over 800,000 linear feet of bank 

[4:7:66,155] protection, over 60 mitigation and environmental restoration sites, and 55 various flood control structures including dams, weirs, 

[4:8:66,165] pumping plants, diversion structures, gate structures, and drop structures. In general, these facilities provide more flood 

[4:9:66,175] protection in urbanized areas and less in agricultural areas.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   245 

[1:0:159,43] 3875   Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy 

[2:0:66,64] The mission of the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta Conservancy is to support efforts that advance both environmental 

[3:0:66,74] protection and the economic well-being of Delta residents in a complementary manner. The Conservancy's activities include 

[3:1:66,84] protecting and enhancing habitat and habitat restoration; protecting and preserving Delta agriculture and working landscapes; 

[3:2:66,95] providing increased opportunities for tourism and recreation; promoting Delta legacy communities and economic vitality in the 

[3:3:66,105] Delta; increasing the resilience of the Delta to the effects of natural disasters such as floods and earthquakes; protecting and 

[3:4:66,115] improving water quality; assisting the Delta regional economy; identifying priority projects and initiatives for which funding is 

[3:5:66,125] needed; conducting activities to protect, conserve, and restore the region's physical, agricultural, cultural, historical, and living 

[3:6:66,135] resources; assisting local entities in the implementation of their habitat conservation plans and natural community conservation 

[3:7:66,146] plans; facilitating protection and safe harbor agreements under the federal Endangered Species Act of 1973 and the California 

[3:8:66,156] Endangered Species Act for adjacent land owners and local public agencies; and promoting environmental education. The 

[4:0:66,166] Conservancy acts as a primary state agency to implement ecosystem restoration in the Delta and supports efforts that advance 

[4:1:66,176] environmental protection and the economic well-being of the Delta residents.

[0:0:66,13] NATURAL RESOURCES   2018-19 STATE BUDGET — RES   249 

[1:0:211,43] 3885   Delta Stewardship Council 

[2:0:66,64] Established in 2009 by the Delta Reform Act, the mission of the Delta Stewardship Council, through a seven-member board, is 

[2:1:66,74] to further the state's coequal goals of providing a more reliable water supply for California and protecting, restoring, and 

[3:0:66,84] enhancing the Sacramento-San Joaquin Delta ecosystem. The coequal goals are to be achieved in a manner that protects and 

[3:1:66,95] enhances the unique cultural, recreational, natural resource, and agricultural values of the Delta. In 2013, the Council adopted 

[3:2:66,105] a legally enforceable Delta Plan to further the coequal goals and guide state and local agency activities related to the Delta. 

[3:3:66,115] Under state law, agencies are required to coordinate their actions pursuant to the Delta Plan with the Council and the other 

[3:4:66,125] relevant agencies. The Council is informed by scientific input from the Delta Science Program and the Delta Independent 

[3:5:66,135] Science Board. The mission of the Delta Science Program is to provide the best possible unbiased scientific information to 

[3:6:66,146] inform water and environmental decisionmaking in the Delta.
